15 min
When Assumption Waives the Order of Missed Prayers
Intra-Hanafi dispute on assumption as an excuse for order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid assumption (dhann mu'tabar) and an invalid one in the context of waiving the order of missed prayers, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving mujtahids and muqallids.

Core Idea
For a mujtahid, a valid assumption that tartib is not wajib waives the order; for a muqallid, it does not unless it coincides with his imam's madhhab.

The order (tartib) between missed and current prayers is a wajib obligation in the Hanafi school, but it is waived by forgetfulness (nisyan) as a divine excuse. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq extends the excuse to a valid assumption (dhann) of non-obligation, but only for a mujtahid. For a muqallid, his personal assumption contrary to his imam's ruling is not considered—unless it happens to align with the madhhab. The author also critically reviews the intra-Hanafi dispute about which time frame determines the need for order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State that the act is the order (tartib) between a missed prayer and the current prayer. The ruling is that tartib is wajib, but it is waived by forgetfulness (nisyan).
The passage begins by affirming the general rule and the exception.
2
Understand the extension to assumption (dhann)
Assumption (dhann) is included with forgetfulness as an excuse. However, not all assumptions are valid—only those of a mujtahid who, through ijtihad, concludes that tartib is not obligatory.
A muqallid's assumption opposing his imam is not a valid excuse.
3
Distinguish between strong and weak invalidity
The commentators of al-Hidayah differentiate between strong invalidity (e.g., lack of purity) which invalidates subsequent prayers, and weak invalidity (e.g., lack of order) which does not. This affects whether a prayer prayed without order due to assumption is valid.
Two branches illustrate: lack of purity vs. lack of order.
4
Apply to mujtahid vs. muqallid
A mujtahid's assumption is a valid excuse because he follows his own ijtihad. A muqallid of Abu Hanifa cannot rely on his own opinion if it contradicts his imam—unless it coincides with the madhhab.
The author refutes earlier scholars who did not make this distinction clearly.
5
Evaluate the author's final position
The author insists that the truth is clear: a mujtahid's assumption is valid, and a muqallid's is not. He also supports the view that the consideration for order is asl al-waqt (beginning of time) over al-waqt al-mustahab (preferred time).
This is the mufta bihi position.

قوله : (والحق أن المجتهد لا كلام فيه أصلاً) رد لما في الكشف وقوله «وإن كان مقلداً الخ» رد لما ذكره الشارحون قوله : فلا عبرة برأيه المخالف لمذهب إمامه قال في النهر : فيه نظر إذ كون هذا الظن لا عبرة به لمخالفته لرأي إمامه في حيز المنع ، وكيف يكون مخالفاً له وقد اعتبره وحينئذ فإفتاء الحنفي بإعادة المغرب غير صحيح ا.هـ. وحاصله أن ما ذكروه من صحة المغرب حين ظنه عدم وجوب الترتيب هو مذهب إمامه لأنه قد اعتبر ظنه وحكم بصحة صلاته فالإفتاء بعدمها مخالف له فلا يكون صحيحاً. هذا معنى كلام النهر وبه سقط قول الشيخ إسماعيل بعد نقله كلام النهر فيه كلام إذ الفرض كونه مقلداً وعمله برأيه خروج عما هو بصدده من التقليد فلا يعتبر على أن قوله وكيف يكون الخ» لا يظهر له معنى صحيح فليتأمل ا.هـ. إذ قد علمت أن عمله قد صادف رأي
Book of Prayer, Chapter on Making up Missed Prayers. The sun then mentioned Zuhr passed in Asr. He said: This is a text that the consideration is the preferred time. Then at that point the difference of the mashayikh ceased because the issue where it is not mentioned in the zahir al-riwaya and is established in another narration, resorting to it is determined. In al-Mujtaba: If he cannot perform the current prayer except with leniency in shortening recitation and actions, then he arranges and restricts to the minimum that suffices for prayer. His statement: (And forgetfulness) meaning the order is waived by forgetfulness, which is not remembering something at its time of need, and it is a heavenly excuse that removes obligation because it is not within his capacity, and because the time for the missed prayer is by remembrance, and what he does not remember is not a time for it. Included with forgetfulness is assumption, so it is not a fourth remover as might be understood. It is of two types: valid and invalid. Their expressions differ regarding it. In Kashf al-Asrar, the explanation of Usul Fakhr al-Islam, that assumption is only valid if the man is a mujtahid; it has appeared to him that observing order is not obligatory – it is a legal evidence like forgetfulness. But if he is remembering and has an illusion while not being a mujtahid, his mere assumption is not a legal evidence. So he considered only the assumption of the mujtahid. The commentators of al-Hidayah like the author of al-Nihaya and Fath al-Qadir stated that if the invalidity of the prayer is strong, like lack of purity, it follows the subsequent prayer; if it is weak, like lack of order, it does not follow. They derived two branches: one, if he prayed Zuhr without purity then prayed Asr while remembering it, he must repeat Asr because the invalidity of Zuhr is strong due to lack of purity, so it necessitates the invalidity of Asr even if he assumed order was not obligatory. Second, if he prayed this Zuhr after this Asr and did not repeat Asr until he prayed Maghrib while remembering it, then Maghrib is valid if he assumed order was not obligatory because the invalidity of Asr is weak due to some imams saying its non-existence, so it does not necessitate invalidity of Maghrib. Imam al-Asbijabi mentioned his assumption is valid. He said: If he prays while remembering the missed prayer and believes it suffices him, then look: if the missed prayer's repetition is agreed upon, he repeats the prayer he prayed while remembering it; if according to us repetition is required, he repeats; according to some scholars, he does not have to repeat since he believes it suffices. He mentioned the two branches. The author explained in Sharh al-Majma' for the second branch that the preventer of permissibility is that the missed prayer is certainly left, so the text requiring observance of order does not apply because it is specific to what is certainly left. The truth is that the mujtahid is not disputed at all, absolutely, whether that missed prayer requires agreed-upon repetition or not, because he is not bound by the ijtihad of Abu Hanifa or others. But if he is a muqallid of Abu Hanifa, then his opinion opposing his imam's madhhab is not considered. The statement in al-Dhahiriyya that what is in al-Muntaga is a clear text that the consideration is the preferred time – there is apparent consideration because what is in al-Muntaga is not disputed on two views. As for considering the preferred time, it is apparent; as for considering the beginning of time, its condition is that the missed prayer does not occur at the time of solar alteration because that time is only valid for the Asr of that day as you know from Qadikhan's expression. So the gist is that what the author mentioned here is unrefined, and others followed him even al-'Ala'i commentator of al-Tanwir, and I have not seen anyone draw attention to what I said, so seize this refinement. And praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds. His statement: (And the truth is that the mujtahid is not disputed at all) is a refutation of what is in Kashf. And his statement: (And if he is a muqallid etc.) is a refutation of what the commentators mentioned. His statement: So his opinion opposing his imam's madhhab is not considered. He said in al-Nahr: There is consideration, for this assumption being not considered due to opposition to his imam's opinion is subject to prohibition. How can it be opposing when he considers it? And then the Hanafi's fatwa of repeating Maghrib is not correct. Its gist is that what they mentioned of the validity of Maghrib upon his assumption of non-obligation of order is his imam's madhhab because he considered his assumption and ruled his prayer valid, so the fatwa of its invalidity is opposing and thus not correct. This is the meaning of al-Nahr's statement, and by it the statement of Sheikh Isma'il after quoting al-Nahr falls – there is speech, for the assumption is he is a muqallid and his acting on his opinion is a departure from what he is doing of taqlid, so it is not considered. Moreover, his statement 'And how can it be etc.' no correct meaning appears, so let it be considered. For you have known that his action coincided with the opinion.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the waiving of the order (tartib) between missed and current prayers when the person has a valid assumption (dhann mu'tabar) that the order is not obligatory. The author begins by clarifying the dispute about whether the consideration for the order is the beginning of the time (asl al-waqt) or the preferred time (al-waqt al-mustahab). He argues, citing several authorities, that the correct view is asl al-waqt, and then shows the implications for when the order is waived. The main issue is that forgetfulness (nisyan) is an excuse that removes the obligation of order, and a valid assumption of non-obligation is included in this. However, only the assumption of a mujtahid is considered; a muqallid's opposing assumption is not valid. The author then recounts two branches from the commentators of al-Hidayah to illustrate the difference between strong invalidity (like lack of purity) and weak invalidity (like lack of order) and their effect on subsequent prayers. Finally, he critiques the earlier scholars' positions and affirms the truth that a mujtahid's assumption is valid, while a muqallid's assumption is not, with the caveat that if the muqallid's assumption coincides with the madhhab, it may be acceptable. The passage ends with a strong assertion that the author's refinement is unique and should be heeded.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartib): The obligation to pray missed prayers in order before the current prayer, unless an excuse exists.
الظن المعتبر (al-dhann al-mu'tabar): A valid assumption based on ijtihad that tartib is not obligatory; considered a legal excuse for a mujtahid.
النسيان (al-nisyan): Forgetfulness that excused the obligation of tartib, as it is a divine excuse.
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): A scholar who performs ijtihad to derive rulings from the sources; his assumption based on ijtihad is legally valid.
المقلد (al-muqallid): A non-mujtahid who follows a madhhab; his personal opinion opposing his imam is not a valid excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following persons has a valid excuse to skip the order (tartib) between missed and current prayers?
A) A muqallid who sincerely believes that tartib is not wajib, based on his own reasoning.
B) A mujtahid who through ijtihad concludes that tartib is not wajib.
C) A person who simply forgot the missed prayer at the time of praying the current prayer.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid's valid assumption that tartib is not wajib exempts him from the order, but a muqallid's assumption is only valid if it coincides with his imam's madhhab.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
12 min
When Does Assumption Excuse the Order of Missed Prayers?
Analyzing when personal assumption excuses the obligation of order in missed prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish strong vs. weak invalidation of prior prayers and apply the Hanafi rule that only a mujtahid's assumption excuses the order requirement.

Core Idea
A mujtahid's assumption that order is not obligatory is a valid excuse; a layperson's is not.

In Hanafi fiqh, maintaining the order (tartib) of missed prayers is wajib, but this obligation can be excused under certain conditions. The key question is: whose assumption (zann) about the non-obligation of order is considered legally valid? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq navigates the distinction between strong and weak invalidation of prior prayers and refines the position that only the assumption of a qualified mujtahid serves as a valid excuse, while a layperson's mere opinion does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline rule
Understand that tartib (order) in making up missed prayers is wajib in the Hanafi school. However, this obligation can be excused by a valid excuse such as forgetfulness or a valid assumption (zann) that it is not obligatory.
The text emphasizes that the excuse applies only when the assumption is from a mujtahid.
2
Distinguish strong vs. weak invalidation
Learn the critical distinction: if a prior prayer is invalidated due to a strong cause (e.g., lack of purity), that invalidation carries over to subsequent prayers. If the invalidation is weak (e.g., lack of tartib), it does not affect subsequent prayers.
This distinction is used to determine whether a later prayer needs to be repeated.
3
Apply the two branches (furu')
Work through the two illustrative examples: (1) Zuhr without purity followed by Asr → Asr is invalid and must be repeated. (2) Zuhr after Asr out of order, then Maghrib → Maghrib remains valid if the person assumed tartib was not obligatory.
In the second branch, the key is that the invalidation of Asr is weak; this is based on the view of some imams that tartib is not obligatory.
4
Recognize the refinement: only mujtahid's zann counts
The author refines the earlier commentators' position: the assumption (zann) that excuses tartib is only that of a mujtahid. For a layperson (muqallid) who follows Abu Hanifa, a contrary opinion is not considered. The truth is that there is no dispute about the mujtahid—his assumption is valid absolutely.
This refinement is presented as the correct view (tahrir).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يعتبر ليست بفرض فهو دليل شرعي كالنسيان، وأما إذا كان الرجل مجتهداً قد ظهر عنده أن مراعاة الترتيب ليست بفرض فهو دليل شرعي كالنسيان، فأما إذا كان ذاكراً وهم وهو غير مجتهد فمجرد ظنه ليس بدليل شرعي اهـ. فجعل المعتبر ظن المجتهد لا غيره، وذكر شارحو الهداية كصاحب النهاية وفتح القدير أن فساد الصلاة إن كان قوياً كعدم الطهارة استتبع الصلاة التي بعده، وإن كان ضعيفاً كعدم الترتيب لا يستتبع وفرعوا على ذلك فرعين: أحدهما لو صلى الظهر بغير طهارة ثم صلى العصر ذاكراً لها وجب عليه إعادة العصر لأن فساد الظهر قوي لعدم الطهارة فأوجب فساد العصر وإن ظن عدم وجوب الترتيب . ثانيهما لو صلى هذه الظهر بعد هذه العصر ولم يعد العصر حتى صلى المغرب ذاكراً لها فالمغرب صحيحة إذا ظن عدم وجوب الترتيب لأن فساد العصر ضعيف لقول بعض الأئمة : بعدمه فلا يستتبع فساد المغرب . وذكر الإمام الأسبيجابي ظنه معتبر وجب له أصلاً فقال : إذا صلى وهو ذاكر للفائتة وهو يرى أنه يجزئه فإنه ينظر إن كانت الفائتة ) إعادتها بالإجماع أعاد التي صلى وهو ذاكر لها وإن كان عليه الإعادة عندنا. وفي قول بعض العلماء ليس عليه وهو يرى أن ذلك يجزئه فلا إعادة عليه وذكر الفرعين المذكورين. وعلل في شرح المجمع للمصنف للفرع الثاني بأن المانع من الجواز كون الفائتة متروكة بيقين فلم يتناولها النص المقتضى لمراعاة الترتيب لاختصاصه بالمتروك بيقين، والحق أن المجتهد لا كلام فيه أصلاً وإن مطلقاً، كانت تلك الفائتة سواء ك وجب إعادتها بالإجماع أو لا، إذ لا يلزمه اجتهاد أبي حنيفة ولا غيره وإن كان مقلداً فإن كان مقلداً لأبي حنيفة فلا عبرة برأيه المخالف لمذهب إمامه قول الظهيرية أن ما في المنتقى نص صريح في أن العبرة للوقت المستحب فيه نظر ظاهر لأن ما في المنتفى لا خلاف فيه على القولين، أما على اعتبار الوقت المستحب فظاهر وأما على اعتبار أصل الوقت فلأن شرطه أن لا تقع الفائتة في وقت تغير الشمس لأن ذلك الوقت لا يصح فيه إلا عصر يومه كما علمت من عبارة قاضيخان التي ذكرناها. فالحاصل أن ما ذكره المؤلف ههنا غير محرر وإن تبعه من بعده عليه حتى العلائي شارح التنوير ولم أر من نبه على ما قلته فاغتنم هذا التحرير والحمد لله رب العالمين.
So it is not considered, it is not an obligation, so it is a legal proof like forgetfulness. And if a man is a mujtahid and it has appeared to him that maintaining order is not an obligation, then it is a legal proof like forgetfulness. But if he is remembering and doubts while he is not a mujtahid, then his mere assumption is not a legal proof. So he made the assumption of the mujtahid valid, not others. And the commentators of al-Hidayah, like the author of al-Nihayah and Fath al-Qadir, mentioned that if the invalidation of prayer is strong, like lack of purity, it affects the prayer after it; if it is weak, like lack of order, it does not affect. And they derived two branches: one: if he prayed Zuhr without purity, then prayed Asr while remembering it, he must repeat Asr because the invalidation of Zuhr is strong due to lack of purity, so it caused the invalidation of Asr even if he assumed that order was not obligatory. Second: if he prayed this Zuhr after this Asr and did not repeat Asr until he prayed Maghrib while remembering it, then Maghrib is valid if he assumed that order was not obligatory, because the invalidation of Asr is weak due to the saying of some imams that it is not required, so it does not cause the invalidation of Maghrib. And al-Imam al-Asbijabi mentioned that his assumption is valid and obligatory for him, and said: if he prays while remembering the missed prayer and he thinks that it suffices him, then one looks: if the missed prayer is unanimously required to be repeated, he repeats what he prayed while remembering it; if according to us it requires repetition, he repeats; according to some scholars he does not, and he thinks that suffices him, so no repetition. And he mentioned the two branches. And in Sharh al-Majma' by the author, he gave as reason for the second branch that the preventing factor is that the missed prayer is certainly abandoned, so the text requiring order does not apply to it because it is specific to what is certainly abandoned. And the truth is that there is no dispute about the mujtahid at all, in an absolute sense, whether that missed prayer requires repetition by consensus or not, because he is not bound by the ijtihad of Abu Hanifa or others. And if he is a muqallid, then if he is a muqallid of Abu Hanifa, his opinion contrary to the school of his imam is not considered. The saying of al-Dhahiriyyah that what is in al-Muntaqa is an explicit text that the consideration is for the preferred time has clear objection, because what is in al-Muntaqa is undisputed on both opinions. As for considering the preferred time, it is obvious; as for considering the actual time, because its condition is that the missed prayer should not occur at the time of the sun's change, because that time is not valid except for the Asr of that day, as you know from the statement of Qadikhan which we mentioned. So the conclusion is that what the author mentioned here is not refined, even though subsequent authors followed him, even al-'Ala'i, the commentator of al-Tanwir, and I have not seen anyone point out what I have said, so seize this refinement. And praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof discussion on the issue of order (tartib) in making up missed prayers. The author begins by citing a previous view that if a person assumes (zann) that tartib is not obligatory, this assumption is considered a legal proof only if he is a mujtahid (one qualified to exercise ijtihad); for a non-mujtahid, such assumption is not valid. Then, quoting the commentators of al-Hidayah (like the author of al-Nihayah and Fath al-Qadir), he distinguishes between strong and weak invalidation of a prior prayer. If a prior prayer is invalid due to a strong cause (e.g., lack of purity), this invalidation carries over to subsequent prayers; if due to a weak cause (e.g., lack of tartib), it does not. Two illustrative examples (furu') are given: (1) If one prays Zuhr without purity (strong invalidation) and then prays Asr, the Asr is also invalid and must be repeated, even if he assumed tartib was not obligatory. (2) If one prays Zuhr after Asr out of order (weak invalidation due to tartib) and then prays Maghrib before repeating Asr, the Maghrib remains valid if he assumed tartib was not obligatory, because the invalidation of Asr (due to missing tartib) is weak and does not affect Maghrib. The author then cites al-Imam al-Asbijabi who considers the assumption valid for the mujtahid but not for others. The author then critiques a justification from Sharh al-Majma' and declares that the correct view is that there is no dispute about the mujtahid—his assumption is valid absolutely. However, for the muqallid of Abu Hanifa, his contrary opinion to his imam is not considered. The author concludes that the earlier commentators' treatment is not well-founded and offers his own refinement (tahrir).

Key Terms
ظن (zann): An assumption or belief that falls short of certainty; in this context, a personal opinion that order (tartib) is not obligatory.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): The process of a qualified scholar deriving a ruling from the sources; here, the mujtahid's assumption is considered a valid legal proof.
ترتيب (tartib): The order of making up missed prayers, i.e., that one should pray the missed prayers in the sequence they were missed.
فساد الصلاة (fasad al-salah): Invalidity of prayer; distinguished as strong (e.g., lack of purity) or weak (e.g., lack of tartib) affect subsequent prayers differently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if someone prays Zuhr without purity (strong invalidation) and then prays Asr while remembering that Zuhr was invalid, but assuming that order is not obligatory?
A) Asr is valid because his assumption excuses him from order.
B) Asr is invalid and must be repeated because the strong invalidation of Zuhr carries over to Asr.
C) Asr is invalid but does not need repetition because of his assumption.
D) Asr is valid only if he is a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, order (tartib) is wajib but excused by a valid excuse (forgetfulness or mujtahid's assumption); strong invalidation carries over while weak does not.

Salah — Prayer

The Assumption of a Muqallid and Layman Regarding Order of Missed Prayers
Determining the validity of prayers for a muqallid or layman who makes an erroneous assumption about order of missed prayers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a muqallid or layman's mistaken assumption excuses the obligation of order in missed prayers, and apply the Hanafi tarjih to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A layman's prayer is valid if his assumption coincides with a valid ijtihad, even if he is wrong.

In Hanafi fiqh, the order of missed prayers (tartib) is wajib, but this obligation can be excused under certain conditions. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the intricate case of a muqallid (follower of a school) or a layman ('ammi) who mistakenly believes order is not required. Understanding when such a mistaken assumption (zann) saves the prayer is crucial for issuing correct fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of persons
The text distinguishes three types: the mujtahid (no dispute), the muqallid (follower of a specific imam), and the 'ammi (layman with no fixed madhhab). Recognize that each has different rules regarding their personal opinion.
The muqallid's assumption is only considered if it coincides with his imam's ruling; the 'ammi's assumption is judged by his mufti's fatwa or by whether it matches a valid ijtihad.
2
Determine the wajib of tartib
Recall that tartib (order) in missed prayers is wajib in the Hanafi school. However, its omission does not invalidate the prayer if there is a valid excuse, such as ignorance that coincides with a valid opinion.
The Hanafi position is that tartib is wajib, not fard, hence it can be excused by a valid zann.
3
Analyze the role of assumption (zann)
The text discusses when a mistaken assumption (e.g., thinking tartib is not required) can excuse. The key is whether the assumption coincides with a matter subject to ijtihad (fasl mujtahad fihi). The Hanafi principle is that if the assumption aligns with a valid ijtihad (like the Shafi'i view that tartib is not wajib), then the prayer is valid.
This is supported by the qiyas analogy from qisas: a mistaken assumption can prevent execution even though the action is technically wrong.
4
Apply the distinction between primary and secondary ijtihad issues
From Fath al-Qadir, the text highlights a subtlety: the mistaken assumption is only valid if it concerns a secondary issue (here, the effect of missing tartib on Maghrib) rather than the primary issue (the invalidity of Asr). This explains why a muqallid's prayer might be valid even though he was wrong about the primary ruling.
The primary issue (invalidity of Asr) is the object of ijtihad; the secondary (validity of Maghrib) is derivative. The assumption about the secondary is considered due to increased weakness.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi based on tarjih
The author sides with al-Nahr and al-'Inayah, concluding that a Hanafi muqallid who assumes tartib is not obligatory should not be ordered to repeat prayers because his assumption is valid within the school. The earlier commentators' opposing view is based on a misunderstanding; the correct position is transmitted from Muhammad al-Shaybani.
Thus, the mufta bihi is that if a Hanafi muqallid holds such an assumption, his Maghrib is valid and no repetition is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والحق أن المجتهد لا كلام فيه أصلاً) رد لما في الكشف وقوله «وإن كان مقلداً الخ» رد لما ذكره الشارحون قوله : فلا عبرة برأيه المخالف لمذهب إمامه قال في النهر : فيه نظر إذ كون هذا الظن لا عبرة به لمخالفته لرأي إمامه في حيز المنع ، وكيف يكون مخالفاً له وقد اعتبره وحينئذ فإفتاء الحنفي بإعادة المغرب غير صحيح ا.هـ. وحاصله أن ما ذكروه من صحة المغرب حين ظنه عدم وجوب الترتيب هو مذهب إمامه لأنه قد اعتبر ظنه وحكم بصحة صلاته فالإفتاء بعدمها مخالف له فلا يكون صحيحاً. هذا معنى كلام النهر وبه سقط قول الشيخ إسماعيل بعد نقله كلام النهر فيه كلام إذ الفرض كونه مقلداً وعمله برأيه خروج عما هو بصدده من التقليد فلا يعتبر على أن قوله وكيف يكون الخ» لا يظهر له معنى صحيح فليتأمل ا.هـ. إذ قد علمت أن عمله قد صادف رأي فيلزمه إعادة المغرب أيضاً، وإن كان مقلداً للشافعي فلا يلزمه إعادة العصر أيضاً، وإن كان عاميا ليس له مذهب معين فمذهبه فتوى مفتيه كما صرحوا به، فإن أفتاه حنفي أعاد العصر والمغرب، وإن أفتاه شافعي فلا يعيدهما ولا عبرة برأيه، وإن لم يستفت أحداً وصادف الصحة على مذهب مجتهد أجزأه ولا إعادة عليه، ويدل عليه ما ذكره في الخلاصة معزياً إلى الفتاوي الصغرى : رجل يرى التيمم إلى الرسغ والوتر ركعة ثم رأى التيمم إلى المرفق والوتر ثلاثاً لا يعيد ما صلى، وإن فعل عن جهل من غير أن يسأل أحداً ثم سأل فأمر بالثلاث يعيد ما صلى شفعوي المذهب إذا صار حنفياً وقد فاتته صلوات في وقت كان شفعوياً، ثم أراد أن يقضيها في الوقت الذي صار حنفياً مع إمامه وكيف يصح الإفتاء بإعادة المغرب، وقد نصوا على عدمها وليس ما ذكره الشراح من الفرعين تفريعاً برأيهم إذ المسألة مذكورة في غير ما كتاب كشرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان وغيره. وذكر في الذخيرة أنها مروية عن محمد رواها عنه ابن سماعة في نوادره . وعزاها في التاتارخانية إلى الأصل وقال في أثنائها : فإن أعاد الظهر وحدها ثم صلى المغرب وهو يظن أن العصر له جائز قال : يجزئه المغرب ويعيد العصر فقط، ولو كان عنده أن العصر لا يجزئه لا يجوز له المغرب. نص عليه ابن سماعة عن محمد ا هـ . وظاهر كلام المؤلف الطعن في ذلك حيث لم يجعله مصوراً بصورة أنه منقول في المذهب كما علمت وقد تابع المؤلف الشرنبلالي في إمداد الفتاح لكنه قال : فتعين حمل المسألة على عامي ليس له مذهب ولم يستفت أحدا فصلاته صحيحة لمصادفتها مجتهداً فيه فلا يتعرض له من علم حاله من غير استفتائه اهـ. وهو بعيد إذ لا فرق حينئذ بين المسألتين فيقتضي أن لا تفسد العصر في المسألة الأولى أيضاً لمصادفته فصلاً مجتهداً فيه لأن الشافعي لا يقول بوجوبه أيضاً في هذه الصورة فتعين حمل المسألة مقلد لأبي حنيفة جهل هذا الحكم ثم استفتى حنيفاً فإنه لا يأمره بإعادة المغرب اعتبار الظنه قال في العناية: الظن متى لاقى فصلاً مجتهداً فيه وقع معتبراً وإن كان خطأ والترتيب لا يوجبه الشافعي رحمه الله فكان ظنه موافقاً لرأيه وصار كما إذ عفا أحد من له القصاص وظن صاحبه أن عفو صاحبه غير مؤثر في حقه فقتل ذلك القاتل لا يقتص منه، ومعلوم أن هذا قتل بغير حق لكن لما كان متأوّلاً ومجتهداً في ذلك صار ذلك الظن مانعاً وجوب القصاص. كذا في المبسوط ا هـ . لكن قوله الظن متى لاقي فصلاً مجتهداً فيه الخ» يتوقف على ما حققه في فتح القدير ليناسب ما نحن فيه وهو أن كون المحل مجتهداً فيه لا يستلزم اعتبار الظن المخطيء فيه من الجاهل، بل إن كان المجتهد فيه ابتداء لا يعتبر الظن وإن كان مما يبني على المجتهد ويستتبعه اعتبر ذلك الظن لزيادة الضعف، ففساد العصر هو المجتهد فيه ابتداء وفساد المغرب بسبب ذلك فاعتبر ا.هـ. وفيه تصريح بأن محل اعتبار هذا الظن وعدمه في الجاهل لا العالم بوجوب الترتيب كما يأتي عن القدوري الكبير، وأما ما سيأتي أيضاً من أنه لم يفرق في الأصل بين العالم والجاهل. وقال في البناية : إنه ظاهر الرواية فقد قال في النهر : إنه مشكل. مجرد
His saying: (And the truth is that there is no dispute about the mujtahid at all) is a refutation of what is in al-Kashshaf. And his saying: 'And if he is a muqallid, etc.' is a refutation of what the commentators mentioned. His saying: 'So his opinion contrary to the school of his imam is not considered' – he said in al-Nahr: there is objection, because the claim that this assumption is not considered due to its opposition to his imam's opinion is subject to denial. How can it be contrary to him when he has considered it? And therefore, the Hanafi's fatwa to repeat Maghrib is not correct. The summary is that what they mentioned about the validity of Maghrib when he assumed that order was not obligatory is the school of his imam, because he (the imam) considered his assumption and ruled his prayer valid; so the fatwa to repeat it is contrary to him and thus not correct. This is the meaning of the words of al-Nahr, and with it the saying of Shaykh Isma'il falls, after quoting the words of al-Nahr, there is discussion, because the assumption is that he is a muqallid and his acting on his opinion is a departure from what he is engaged in of taqlid, so it is not considered. Moreover, his saying 'And how can it be, etc.' does not appear to have a correct meaning, so let it be considered. Since you know that his action coincided with the opinion, so he must repeat Maghrib as well. And if he is a muqallid of al-Shafi'i, then he is not obliged to repeat Asr as well. And if he is a layman (ammi) with no specific madhhab, then his madhhab is the fatwa of his mufti, as they have stated. If a Hanafi mufti gives him a fatwa, he repeats Asr and Maghrib; if a Shafi'i mufti gives him a fatwa, he does not repeat either, and his own opinion is not considered. And if he did not consult anyone and his prayer coincided with a valid opinion on the madhhab of a mujtahid, it suffices him and there is no repetition. And proof of this is what is mentioned in al-Khulasah, attributing to al-Fatawa al-Sughra: A man who considers tayammum to the wrist and witr as one rak'ah, then he considers tayammum to the elbow and witr as three – he does not repeat what he prayed. But if he acted out of ignorance without asking anyone, then he asked and was commanded to do three, he repeats what he prayed. A Shafi'i-madhhab person who becomes Hanafi and has missed prayers during the time he was Shafi'i, then wishes to make them up in the time he became Hanafi, with his imam. How can the fatwa to repeat Maghrib be correct, when they have explicitly stated it? And what the commentators mentioned of the two branches is not their own deduction, because the issue is mentioned in many books, like Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan and others. And it is mentioned in al-Dhakhirah that it is narrated from Muhammad, transmitted by Ibn Sam'ah in his Nawadir. And al-Tatar Khaniyyah attributes it to al-Asl, and says in its midst: If he repeated Zuhr alone, then prayed Maghrib while assuming that Asr was permissible for him, he said: Maghrib suffices him and he only repeats Asr. And if he thought that Asr was not permissible for him, Maghrib is not valid. This is stated by Ibn Sam'ah from Muhammad. Apparently, the author's statement impugns this, as he did not present it as transmitted in the school. And the author followed al-Shirnbalali in Imdad al-Fattah, but he said: So the issue must be interpreted as concerning a layman without a madhhab who did not consult anyone, and his prayer is valid because it coincides with a valid opinion; so one who knows his situation should not interfere without his asking. But this is far-fetched, because then there is no difference between the two issues; it would imply that Asr in the first issue is also not invalidated because it coincides with a valid opinion, since al-Shafi'i also does not require it in this form. So the issue must be interpreted as a muqallid of Abu Hanifa who was ignorant of this ruling and then consulted a Hanafi mufti, and he does not order him to repeat Maghrib due to considering his assumption. He said in al-'Inayah: Whenever an assumption coincides with a matter that is subject to ijtihad, it is considered valid even if mistaken. And order is not required by al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, so his assumption agreed with his opinion; and it becomes like the case when one of the heirs pardons and his companion assumes that the pardon of his companion does not affect his right, so he kills the killer – the killer is not subject to retribution. And it is known that this is killing without right, but because he had a valid interpretation and was a mujtahid in that matter, that assumption prevented the obligation of retribution. Thus in al-Mabsut. But his saying 'Whenever an assumption coincides with a matter that is subject to ijtihad, etc.' depends on what is verified in Fath al-Qadir to suit our context, which is that the fact that an issue is subject to ijtihad does not entail that the mistaken assumption of an ignorant person is considered; rather, if the ijtihad is in the primary issue, the assumption is not considered; if it is in what is built upon the ijtihad and follows from it, then that assumption is considered due to increased weakness. So the invalidation of Asr is the primary issue of ijtihad, and the invalidation of Maghrib is due to that, so it is considered. There is explicit statement that the place of considering this assumption or not is for the ignorant person, not for the one who knows the obligation of order, as comes from al-Quduri al-Kabir. And what also comes that he did not differentiate in al-Asl between the knowledgeable and the ignorant. And he said in al-Binayah: It is the apparent of the riwayat. So he said in al-Nahr: It is problematic. Just.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage continues the critical refinement of the ruling on assumption (zann) in the order of missed prayers. The author focuses on the muqallid (follower of a specific madhhab) and the 'ammi (layman without a fixed madhhab). He begins by discussing the previous statement that 'the mujtahid has no dispute,' and then critiques the commentaries that claim a muqallid's opinion contrary to his imam is invalid. Citing al-Nahr, he argues that if a muqallid of Abu Hanifa holds a valid assumption (like thinking tartib is not obligatory), the imam himself (Abu Hanifa) would consider that assumption valid, so a later Hanafi mufti cannot order him to repeat prayers. Then the author outlines three cases: (1) A muqallid of Abu Hanifa who assumes tartib is not obligatory must still repeat Maghrib? Actually, the text states 'فيلزمه إعادة المغرب أيضاً' meaning he must repeat both Asr and Maghrib? That seems contradictory. Later it clarifies: if he is a muqallid of Shafi'i, he does not need to repeat Asr; if he is a layman with no madhhab, he follows his mufti's fatwa. If he did not consult anyone and his prayer happened to coincide with a valid opinion (e.g., Shafi'i), it is valid. The author then cites supporting examples from al-Khulasah about tayammum and witr, and a case from al-Dhakhirah and al-Tatar Khaniyyah. He then discusses a subtle point from Fath al-Qadir: the mistaken assumption of a layman is considered valid only if the ijtihad is in the 'secondary' issue (like the effect on Maghrib), not the primary issue (like the invalidation of Asr). Finally, he notes that there is conflict in the sources: some, like al-Quduri, distinguish between knowledgeable and ignorant, while al-Asl does not, and al-Binayah supports one view while al-Nahr finds it problematic. The text ends abruptly with 'مجرد' (bare/just).

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid): A person who follows a specific madhhab (e.g., Hanafi) and is bound by its rulings; his personal opinion contrary to his imam is not normally valid.
عامي ('ammi): A layperson who has no fixed madhhab; his madhhab is the fatwa of the mufti he consults, or if he doesn't consult, his prayer is valid if it coincides with any valid ijtihad.
فصل مجتهد فيه (fasl mujtahad fihi): A matter that is subject to ijtihad (i.e., there is no definitive text, and scholars may differ). The assumption of a layman that coincides with such a valid opinion is considered.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or most authentic narrations in the Hanafi school; here, al-Binayah states that the ruling (not distinguishing between knowledgeable and ignorant) is from zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the author's final position (mufta bihi) regarding a Hanafi muqallid who prays Maghrib before repeating Asr, mistakenly assuming order is not obligatory?
A) His Maghrib is invalid and he must repeat it.
B) His Maghrib is valid because his assumption coincides with a valid ijtihad (the Shafi'i view).
C) His Maghrib is valid only if he later consults a Hanafi mufti and the mufti says so.
D) His Maghrib is invalid because he is a muqallid and cannot have valid opinions.

Key Takeaway
A muqallid or layman's mistaken assumption about the order of missed prayers can excuse its omission if the assumption coincides with a valid ijtihad, such as the Shafi'i view that tartib is not wajib.

Ruling for a Muqallid and Common Person on Repeating Prayers
Ruling on validity of prayers for muqallid and 'ammi regarding qada' al-fawa'it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of prayers performed by a muqallid or an 'ammi when they act without inquiry or after following a different mufti, and apply the principle that objective coincidence with a valid ijtihad suffices.

Core Idea
A prayer is valid if it matches any valid ijtihad, regardless of the person's madhhab or inquiry.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a crucial question: what happens to past prayers when a person changes their madhhab or follows a different mufti? The Hanafi school provides a clear principle — validity depends on the prayer itself being sound according to some recognized ijtihad, not on the person's subjective state. Let's unpack the ruling for both a muqallid (follower of a madhhab) and an 'ammi (common person with no fixed madhhab).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage lays out three distinct cases: a muqallid of al-Shafi'i, an 'ammi who consults a mufti, and an 'ammi who acts without any consultation. For each, determine whether past prayers need to be repeated.
Note that the muqallid's madhhab is fixed; the 'ammi's madhhab is the fatwa of the mufti he follows.
2
Apply the principle of objective validity
The core principle is that a prayer is valid if it coincides with any valid ijtihad, regardless of the person's own madhhab or knowledge. For the Shafi'i muqallid, his prayer follows his madhhab, so it is valid. For the 'ammi with a Hanafi mufti, his prayer must match Hanafi rulings; if it does not, he must repeat. If he follows a Shafi'i mufti, no repetition.
This shows that the objective standard is the prayer's correctness according to a recognized school, not the person's personal state.
3
Analyze the case of acting without inquiry
If a person prays without consulting a mufti, and his prayer coincidentally matches the view of any mujtahid, it is valid and no repetition is required. This extends the principle to anyone without access to qualified guidance.
This ruling provides flexibility and avoids undue hardship.
4
Examine the proof from al-Khulasa
The passage cites an example from al-Khulasa: a person changes his view on tayammum (from wrist to elbow) and witr (from one to three rak'ahs). If he acted without asking, past prayers are valid. If he later asks and is told a different ruling, he repeats only those after the inquiry.
The example reinforces that ignorance before inquiry does not invalidate past acts, but once he receives guidance, he must follow it.
5
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah)
The thamarah is that a person should not constantly repeat prayers due to changing views or madhhab. However, once he obtains a fatwa, he is bound by it. This balances flexibility with the obligation to follow qualified scholarship.
This principle is particularly relevant in multi-madhhab societies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيلزمه إعادة المغرب أيضاً، وإن كان مقلداً للشافعي فلا يلزمه إعادة العصر أيضاً، وإن كان عاميا ليس له مذهب معين فمذهبه فتوى مفتيه كما صرحوا به، فإن أفتاه حنفي أعاد العصر والمغرب، وإن أفتاه شافعي فلا يعيدهما ولا عبرة برأيه، وإن لم يستفت أحداً وصادف الصحة على مذهب مجتهد أجزأه ولا إعادة عليه، ويدل عليه ما ذكره في الخلاصة معزياً إلى الفتاوي الصغرى : رجل يرى التيمم إلى الرسغ والوتر ركعة ثم رأى التيمم إلى المرفق والوتر ثلاثاً لا يعيد ما صلى، وإن فعل عن جهل من غير أن يسأل أحداً ثم سأل فأمر بالثلاث يعيد ما صلى
Then he must also repeat Maghrib. If he was a follower of al-Shafi'i, he is not required to repeat Asr either. If he is a common person with no specific madhhab, his madhhab is the fatwa of his mufti, as they have stated. If a Hanafi gives him a fatwa, he repeats both Asr and Maghrib; if a Shafi'i gives him a fatwa, he does not repeat either, and there is no consideration to his own opinion. If he did not seek a fatwa from anyone and his action coincidentally coincides with correctness according to a mujtahid's school, then it suffices and there is no repetition required. Evidence for this is what is mentioned in al-Khulasa, referring to al-Fatawa al-Sughra: A man who considers tayammum to the wrist and witr as one rak'ah, then later considers tayammum to the elbow and witr as three, does not repeat what he prayed. But if he acted out of ignorance without asking anyone, then later asked and was ordered to do three, he repeats what he prayed.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling for a person who, after following a Shafi'i mufti or having no fixed madhhab, wonders about the validity of his prayers. The principle is that a muqallid's prayer is valid if it matches the madhhab he follows. For a common person with no madhhab, his madhhab is that of the mufti he consults. If he consults a Hanafi mufti, he must repeat both Asr and Maghrib; if a Shafi'i, he does not. However, if he acts without consulting anyone and his action coincidentally matches the view of a mujtahid, it is valid. The example from al-Khulasa illustrates this: a person who changes his view on tayammum or witr does not repeat past prayers if he acted without asking. But if he later asks and is told a different ruling, he repeats only if he was acting out of ignorance. This shows that the primary factor is whether the prayer is valid in itself according to some valid ijtihad.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid): A person who follows a particular madhhab in matters of fiqh.
عامي (ammi): A common person who does not have a specific madhhab; his madhhab is the fatwa of the mufti he consults.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A common person ('ammi) with no fixed madhhab prays 'Asr without consulting a mufti. Later he realizes his method of prayer matches the Hanafi school. What is the ruling?
A) He must repeat 'Asr because he didn't follow a madhhab.
B) His 'Asr is valid because it coincides with a valid ijtihad.
C) He must repeat 'Asr only if he was aware of the Hanafi ruling.
D) His 'Asr is invalid because he didn't intend to follow Hanafi.

Key Takeaway
A prayer's validity depends on its own correctness according to some recognized ijtihad, not on the person's madhhab affiliation or knowledge.

Mistaken Belief and Prayer Validity in Madhhab Transition
Dispute on repeating Maghrib after madhhab change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a person transitioning from Shafi'i to Hanafi must repeat Maghrib when he mistakenly believed his Asr was valid, and explain the legal principle of zan in ijtihad matters.

Core Idea
A mistaken belief coinciding with a valid ijtihad can excuse one from repeating prayers.

This lesson examines a specific case of inter-madhhab transition: a Shafi'i who becomes Hanafi and has missed prayers. The key question is whether he must repeat Maghrib after making up Zuhr and Asr in sequence, given his mistaken belief that the earlier Asr was valid. The Hanafi school addresses this through a narration from Imam Muhammad and the principle that zan (mistaken belief) is legally considered when it coincides with an ijtihad matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the specific situation: a Shafi'i (who does not require tartib between makeup prayers) becomes Hanafi (who generally requires tartib). He has missed prayers from his Shafi'i period and now wants to make them up as a Hanafi.
2
Frame the dispute
The dispute is about what happens if he prays Maghrib after making up Zuhr, believing that his Asr (which he didn't make up yet) is valid. Must he repeat Maghrib?
3
Examine the narration from Muhammad
According to a narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani via Ibn Sama'a, if he repeats Zuhr and then prays Maghrib while thinking Asr is valid, Maghrib is valid and he only needs to repeat Asr.
This narration is found in Nawadir and cited in works like al-Dhakhira.
4
Analyze the principle of zan
The Hanafi principle states that a mistaken belief (zan) that coincides with an ijtihad matter (mujtahad fih) is legally considered, even if it is wrong. Here, tartib is not obligatory according to the Shafi'i school, so his belief aligns with a valid ijtihad.
5
Apply the analogy from qisas
An analogy from the law of qisas is given: if a person with the right to qisas pardons, and the killer mistakenly thinks the pardon is ineffective and kills, he is not executed because he was exercising ijtihad. Similarly, the mistaken belief here prevents repetition.
This analogy is cited from al-Mabsut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شفعوي المذهب إذا صار حنفياً وقد فاتته صلوات في وقت كان شفعوياً، ثم أراد أن يقضيها في الوقت الذي صار حنفياً مع إمامه وكيف يصح الإفتاء بإعادة المغرب، وقد نصوا على عدمها وليس ما ذكره الشراح من الفرعين تفريعاً برأيهم إذ المسألة مذكورة في غير ما كتاب كشرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان وغيره. وذكر في الذخيرة أنها مروية عن محمد رواها عنه ابن سماعة في نوادره . وعزاها في التاتارخانية إلى الأصل وقال في أثنائها : فإن أعاد الظهر وحدها ثم صلى المغرب وهو يظن أن العصر له جائز قال : يجزئه المغرب ويعيد العصر فقط، ولو كان عنده أن العصر لا يجزئه لا يجوز له المغرب. نص عليه ابن سماعة عن محمد ا هـ . وظاهر كلام المؤلف الطعن في ذلك حيث لم يجعله مصوراً بصورة أنه منقول في المذهب كما علمت وقد تابع المؤلف الشرنبلالي في إمداد الفتاح لكنه قال : فتعين حمل المسألة على عامي ليس له مذهب ولم يستفت أحدا فصلاته صحيحة لمصادفتها مجتهداً فيه فلا يتعرض له من علم حاله من غير استفتائه اهـ. وهو بعيد إذ لا فرق حينئذ بين المسألتين فيقتضي أن لا تفسد العصر في المسألة الأولى أيضاً لمصادفته فصلاً مجتهداً فيه لأن الشافعي لا يقول بوجوبه أيضاً في هذه الصورة فتعين حمل المسألة مقلد لأبي حنيفة جهل هذا الحكم ثم استفتى حنيفاً فإنه لا يأمره بإعادة المغرب اعتبار الظنه قال في العناية: الظن متى لاقى فصلاً مجتهداً فيه وقع معتبراً وإن كان خطأ والترتيب لا يوجبه الشافعي رحمه الله فكان ظنه موافقاً لرأيه وصار كما إذ عفا أحد من له القصاص وظن صاحبه أن عفو صاحبه غير مؤثر في حقه فقتل ذلك القاتل لا يقتص منه، ومعلوم أن هذا قتل بغير حق لكن لما كان متأوّلاً ومجتهداً في ذلك صار ذلك الظن مانعاً وجوب القصاص. كذا في المبسوط ا هـ.
A person of the Shafi'i school who becomes Hanafi and has missed prayers during the time he was Shafi'i, then wants to make them up when he has become Hanafi, with his imam. How can issuing a fatwa for repeating Maghrib be correct when they have stated against it? The two branches mentioned by the commentators are not derived from their own opinion, for the issue is mentioned in more than one book, such as Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadhi Khan and others. Al-Dhakhira mentions that it is narrated from Muhammad, transmitted from him by Ibn Sama'a in his Nawadir. Al-Tatarkhaniyya attributes it to al-Asl and states in its context: 'If he repeats Zuhr alone, then prays Maghrib while thinking that Asr is permissible for him, he said: Maghrib suffices and he only repeats Asr. But if he believes that Asr is not permissible for him, Maghrib is not valid for him. Textually stated by Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad.' The author's statement criticizes this, as he did not frame it as being transmitted in the school. Al-Shurunbulali followed the author in Imdad al-Fattah, but he said: 'The issue must be carried upon a common person who has no madhhab and did not consult anyone; his prayer is valid because it coincides with a mujtahid, so no one who knows his situation should interfere without his consultation.' This is far-fetched, as there is no difference between the two cases; thus Asr would not be invalid in the first case because it also coincides with a mujtahid, since the Shafi'i does not require its obligation in this case. Therefore, the case must be carried upon a follower of Abu Hanifa who was ignorant of this ruling, then consulted a Hanafi mufti; he does not order him to repeat Maghrib out of consideration for his belief. Al-`Inayah said: 'A belief, when it coincides with an ijtihad matter, is considered valid even if wrong. Tartib is not obligatory according to al-Shafi'i, so his belief was in accordance with his view. It becomes like the case where one person with the right to qisas pardons, and his companion thinks that the pardon has no effect, so he kills the killer, and the killer is not subject to qisas.' It is known that this is a murder without right, but since he was interpreting and exercising ijtihad, that belief prevented the obligation of qisas. As in al-Mabsut.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage delves into a specific controversy: a Shafi'i who becomes Hanafi and has missed prayers from his Shafi'i period. The question is whether he must repeat Maghrib after making up Zuhr and Asr in a certain order. The text clarifies that the two branches (furū') that commentators describe are not their own invention but based on a narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani via Ibn Sama'a. The narration states that if he repeats Zuhr alone then prays Maghrib while mistakenly believing Asr is valid, his Maghrib is acceptable and he only needs to repeat Asr. But if he knew Asr was invalid, Maghrib is also invalid. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq criticizes this, implying it is not well-known in the school. The commentator follows with al-Shurunbulali's attempt to reconcile by limiting it to a common person who didn't consult anyone, but this is rejected as inconsistent. The correct understanding is that it applies to a Hanafi follower who was ignorant of the obligation of tartib, then consulted a Hanafi mufti; the mufti does not order repetition because of the validity of his mistaken belief (zan) as per the principle that a mistaken belief coinciding with an ijtihad matter is considered. An analogy from qisas is given: if a pardoning relative mistakenly thinks his pardon is ineffective and kills, he is not executed because he was exercising ijtihad. Thus, the mistaken belief in the validity of Asr, matching the Shafi'i school which does not oblige tartib, saves him from repeating Maghrib.

Key Terms
ظن (zan): A mistaken belief or presumption about a legal ruling.
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fih): A matter that is open to ijtihad (i.e., not explicitly settled by definitive text).
ترتيب (tartib): The obligation to make up missed prayers in their proper sequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a Shafi'i becomes Hanafi and prays Maghrib after Zuhr while mistakenly believing his Asr is valid, what is the ruling according to the narration?
A) He must repeat Maghrib.
B) Maghrib is valid and he only repeats Asr.
C) Both Asr and Maghrib must be repeated.
D) He must repeat everything.

Key Takeaway
A mistaken belief (zan) is legally valid when it coincides with an ijtihad matter, excusing one from repeating prayers that would otherwise be invalid.

Primary vs. Secondary Ijtihad Matters: When Is Mistaken Belief Considered?
Clarifies when a mistaken belief in an ijtihad matter is legally considered, distinguishing primary from secondary issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between primary and secondary ijtihad matters and state the refined Hanafi position on when a mistaken belief (zan) is considered.

Core Idea
Mistaken belief in secondary ijtihad matters is considered; in primary matters, it is not.

The general rule that a mistaken belief coinciding with an ijtihad matter is considered seems straightforward, but Fath al-Qadir introduces a crucial refinement. Not all ijtihad matters are equal: some are primary rulings themselves, while others are secondary consequences. This lesson unpacks that distinction using the example of invalidity in consecutive prayers, clarifying when an ignorant person's belief affects the ruling and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
State the broad principle
Recall that the text discusses the rule: a mistaken belief (zan) is considered when it coincides with an ijtihad matter. This is the starting point.
This principle is found in earlier sections of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
2
Identify the refinement from Fath al-Qadir
Fath al-Qadir argues that the principle applies only when the ijtihad matter is secondary (built upon another ijtihad matter), not when it is primary (the first instance of ijtihad).
The key terms are 'ibtida'an' (primary) and 'yabni 'ala al-mujtahad' (built upon ijtihad).
3
Apply to the Asr/Maghrib example
The invalidity of Asr (e.g., due to broken wudu) is itself a matter of ijtihad (primary). The ignorant person's belief that his Asr is valid is not considered. However, the invalidity of Maghrib arising from the invalidity of Asr is secondary, so the mistaken belief that Maghrib is valid is considered.
This shows the practical difference: Maghrib would be considered valid despite the underlying flaw.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
The passage mentions that al-Asl (likely referring to Imam Muhammad's al-Asl) does not differentiate between primary and secondary, and al-Binaya claims that is the apparent position. This creates a tension within the school.
Al-Nahr notes it is 'problematic' (mushkil).
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The refined view from Fath al-Qadir is relied upon for fatwa, as it resolves the apparent inconsistency and aligns with the purpose of the principle.
The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن قوله الظن متى لاقي فصلاً مجتهداً فيه الخ» يتوقف على ما حققه في فتح القدير ليناسب ما نحن فيه وهو أن كون المحل مجتهداً فيه لا يستلزم اعتبار الظن المخطيء فيه من الجاهل، بل إن كان المجتهد فيه ابتداء لا يعتبر الظن وإن كان مما يبني على المجتهد ويستتبعه اعتبر ذلك الظن لزيادة الضعف، ففساد العصر هو المجتهد فيه ابتداء وفساد المغرب بسبب ذلك فاعتبر ا.هـ. وفيه تصريح بأن محل اعتبار هذا الظن وعدمه في الجاهل لا العالم بوجوب الترتيب كما يأتي عن القدوري الكبير، وأما ما سيأتي أيضاً من أنه لم يفرق في الأصل بين العالم والجاهل. وقال في البناية : إنه ظاهر الرواية فقد قال في النهر : إنه مشكل.
But his statement: 'A belief, when it coincides with an ijtihad matter...' depends on what was verified in Fath al-Qadir to suit our discussion: that the fact that a place is subject to ijtihad does not necessarily entail that the mistaken belief of an ignorant person is considered. Rather, if the ijtihad matter is primary, the belief is not considered; if it is something that builds upon ijtihad and follows from it, then that belief is considered due to the increased weakness. So the invalidity of Asr is the ijtihad matter primarily, while the invalidity of Maghrib is due to that, so it was considered. End quote. And it clearly states that the place for considering this belief or not is in the ignorant person, not the one who knows the obligation of tartib, as will come from al-Quduri al-Kabir. And as will also come that he did not differentiate in al-Asl between the knowledgeable and the ignorant. And al-Binaya said: 'It is the apparent position of the school.' So al-Nahr said: 'It is problematic.'
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from Fath al-Qadir. The commentator uses it to qualify the broad principle that a mistaken belief (zan) is always considered when it coincides with an ijtihad matter. Fath al-Qadir explains that this only applies when the ijtihad matter is a secondary consequence, not a primary ruling. In the example, the invalidity of Asr (tartib) is itself a matter of ijtihad (primary), so the ignorant person's mistaken belief that his Asr is valid is not considered. However, the invalidity of Maghrib is a secondary consequence of the invalidity of Asr, so the belief is considered. This nuance resolves the apparent inconsistency. The passage also notes the difference of opinion: al-Quduri al-Kabir distinguishes between the ignorant and the knowledgeable, while al-Asl does not. Al-Binaya claims the latter is the apparent position, causing confusion.

Key Terms
ابتداء (ibtida'an): Primary, original, as the first instance of ijtihad (i.e., the core issue).
يبنى على المجتهد ويستتبعه (yabni 'ala al-mujtahad wa yastatbi'uhu): Something that builds upon an ijtihad matter and follows from it; a secondary legal consequence.
الظن المخطيء (al-zann al-mukhti'): A mistaken belief or assumption about a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Fath al-Qadir, when is a mistaken belief (zan) in an ijtihad matter considered?
A) Always, if the person is ignorant.
B) Only when the ijtihad matter is primary.
C) Only when the ijtihad matter is secondary.
D) Never, if the person is knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
The refined Hanafi position holds that mistaken belief is considered only in secondary ijtihad matters due to increased weakness, not in primary ones.

Dropping of Tartib Due to Forgetfulness and Six Missed Prayers
States when the obligation of order in making up missed prayers drops: forgetfulness and having six or more missed prayers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the obligation of tartib drops in the Hanafi school and apply the rules to scenarios involving forgetfulness, ignorance, and having six or more missed prayers.

Core Idea
Tartib drops when you forget, are ignorant, or when your missed prayers reach six.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains two key exceptions to the general obligation of tartib in making up missed prayers: forgetfulness (nisyan) and having six or more missed prayers (kathra). It also addresses the status of the ignorant person, who is treated like the forgetful. Understanding these rulings is essential for anyone who has missed several prayers and needs to make them up correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that tartib (order in making up missed prayers) is wajib, but drops in certain circumstances.
Tartib is a condition for the validity of qada when the missed prayers are few and the person remembers.
2
Identify the two main causes
The text lists forgetfulness (nisyan) and becoming many (kathra, defined as six or more) as causes for tartib to drop.
Forgetfulness includes not remembering the missed prayer at the time of the current prayer.
3
Understand the additional cause: ignorance
Al-Mujtaba and many scholars of Balkh treat ignorance of the obligation of tartib like forgetfulness, so tartib drops.
This is the mufta bihi position, though al-Asl makes no distinction.
4
Analyze the threshold for kathra
The correct view is that tartib drops when the missed prayers become six, not when the seventh time enters. The text refutes alternative views.
The passage emphasizes that the benefit of dropping is not limited to the current prayer's validity but also applies among the missed prayers.
5
Apply the rule to scenarios
Practice applying the rules to cases of forgetfulness, ignorance, and kathra to determine whether tartib is required.
Consider the timing of recollection and the number of missed prayers.

His statement: (and its becoming six) means: tartib drops when the missed prayers become six, because they exceed the limit of fewness which would cause hardship if we required its obligation. The exceeding is by entering into repetition, which occurs when the missed prayers are six; this is correct. Thus what was narrated from Muhammad that the entry of the sixth (time) is considered is refuted, and also what is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, Ghayat al-Bayan, and many that the entry of the seventh time is considered to make the missed prayers six, because their becoming six does not depend on the entry of the seventh, as if he misses a full day and the Fajr of the next day, then the missed prayers become six at sunrise of the next day without entering the seventh time. It might be said: since the benefit of dropping is the validity of the current prayer, which only occurs upon entering the seventh time, the seventh time is considered. The answer: the benefit of dropping is not limited to that, because upon entering the seventh time, tartib is not required between the missed prayers either, as will come. The wording of the author (al-Kanz) is better than the wording of al-Hidayah and al-Quduri, where they said: 'unless the missed prayers exceed six prayers' as an exception from his statement 'arrange them in making up', because its apparent meaning implies that the missed prayers are seven according to Fath al-Qadir, or nine according to al-Nihaya. Although Ghayat al-Bayan responds by interpreting the missed prayers as the times metaphorically due to ambiguity, along with what we have mentioned about the non-requirement of entry into the seventh time. Al-Muhit explicitly states that the apparent position of the school is that tartib drops when the missed prayers become six, in agreement with al-Mukhtasar. His statement: (and in al-Mujtaba: whoever is ignorant) is transmitted by Qadhi Khan in his commentary from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, and he said: Many of the mashayikh adopted his saying. Similar in al-Tatarkhaniyya. His statement: (entry of the sixth) means entry of the time of the sixth, as in al-Hidayah. His statement: (and it may be said) regarding if he wants to pray Zuhr of the second day in this case, its performance is valid because tartib drops due to the missed prayers becoming six. His statement: (from the fact that the missed prayers are seven) means with the letter sin (sab') which is apparent. And his statement: 'or nine' means with the letter ta' (tisa'), and the explanation: he mentioned missed prayers in the plural, and the excess is not the one exceeded, the exceeded is six, and the excess missed prayers are three because it is the minimum of plural, so the total becomes nine. But it is not understood from your statement 'these dirhams exceed a hundred' except that their number exceeds a hundred dirhams, and the intended meaning here...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This long passage addresses when the obligation of orderly sequence (tartib) in making up missed prayers is dropped. Two causes are given: forgetfulness (nisyan) and having six or more missed prayers (kathra). The text first distinguishes between the ignorant and the forgetful person. According to al-Mujtaba and scholars of Balkh, ignorance of the obligation is like forgetfulness, so tartib is not required. However, al-Quduri al-Kabir reports a detailed scenario: if someone prays Asr while mindful that he missed Zuhr, his Asr is corrupt; then he makes up Zuhr and prays Maghrib before repeating Asr; his Maghrib is valid if he was unaware that he must repeat Asr, but invalid if he knew. The original (al-Asl) makes no distinction between knowledge and ignorance. The author of the commentary notes that this aligns with the principle 'the corrupt does not necessitate tartib.' Then a narration from al-Nawadir is discussed: if someone prays Zuhr thinking he had wudu, then after performing wudu prays Asr, and later finds out his Zuhr was invalid, he only repeats Zuhr because he is like the forgetful person regarding Zuhr, so tartib is not required. This is distinguished from the earlier case by the timing of recollection. Regarding the six-prayer rule: when the number of missed prayers reaches six, tartib drops. The correct position is that it becomes six at the entry of the sixth prayer time (e.g., sunrise after missing a full day plus Fajr of next day). This refutes the view that the seventh time must enter. An objection based on the benefit of dropping (validity of current prayer) is answered: the benefit also includes not requiring tartib among the missed prayers themselves. The author's wording in Kanz al-Daqa'iq is preferred over al-Hidayah and al-Quduri which state 'exceed six' implying seven or nine missed prayers. The commentary on the margin explains the possible numbers (7 or 9) and clarifies that the intended meaning is six (the exceeded), not the total. Finally, a quote from al-Mujtaba via Qadhi Khan and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad affirms that ignorance of tartib excuses tartib, and many scholars followed this. The phrase 'dakhul al-sadisa' (entry of the sixth) is interpreted as entry of the time of the sixth. A possible confusion about numbers (7 or 9) is resolved by explaining that the plural 'fawa'it' in the phrase 'zadat al-fawa'it 'ala sitt salawat' means the exceeded prayers are six, and the excess (minimally three) makes the total nine, but the common understanding is that the excess is considered relative to six, not the total.

Key Terms
ترتيب (tartib): The obligation to make up missed prayers in chronological order.
نسيان (nisyan): Forgetfulness that causes the dropping of tartib.
كثرة (kathra): The state of having six or more missed prayers, causing hardship and dropping of tartib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many missed prayers must one have for the obligation of tartib to drop?
A) Five.
B) Six.
C) Seven.
D) Three.

Key Takeaway
Tartib drops in three cases: forgetfulness, ignorance of its obligation, and having six or more missed prayers.

Ignorance and Forgetfulness in the Order of Missed Prayers
Explaining that one ignorant of order is like the forgetful and order is waived

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on order of missed prayers when the person is ignorant or forgetful, and apply the principle that an invalid prayer does not necessitate order.

Core Idea
Ignorance and forgetfulness excuse the obligation of order in making up missed prayers.

In the Hanafi school, observing the chronological order (tartib) when making up missed prayers is normally wajib. But what if the person is ignorant of this rule or forgets? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores the nuances, showing that the ignorant and forgetful are treated similarly, based on the maxim that an invalid prayer (fasid) does not create an obligation of order. We will trace the reasoning through key cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is making up missed prayers (qada). The default Hanafi ruling is that order (tartib) is wajib when one knows the missed prayer and remembers it while performing a present prayer.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa.
2
Examine the case from al-Quduri al-Kabir
The text presents a scenario: someone leaves Zuhr and prays Asr while remembering the missed Zuhr. His Asr is invalid (fasid). Then he makes up Zuhr and prays Maghrib before repeating Asr. If he knows he must repeat Asr, his Maghrib is invalid; if he does not know, it is valid.
This illustrates that knowledge of the invalidity matters.
3
Understand the principle: the fasid does not necessitate order
The commentator explains that an invalid prayer (like the Asr in the case) does not create an obligation of order. Therefore, the Maghrib prayed before redoing the invalid Asr can be valid if the person is ignorant.
This is the key legal maxim.
4
Apply the principle to the ignorant and forgetful
The text cites al-Mujtaba: one who is ignorant of the obligation of order is like the forgetful—order is not required. A group of scholars from Balkh held this view.
Ignorance here means genuine unawareness, not willful neglect.
5
Distinguish based on timing of remembrance
The case from al-Nawadir: someone prays Zuhr without wudu (thinking he has wudu), then prays Asr after wudu. Later he realizes Zuhr was invalid. He only repeats Zuhr, and order is not required because the remembrance came after Asr. This does not contradict the earlier case because there the person remembered during the present prayer.
Timing is crucial: if remembrance occurs during the present prayer, order is required; if after, it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب قضاء الفوائت والنسيان وصيرورتها ستاً ولم يعد بعودها إلى القلة فلو صلى فرضاً ذاكراً فائتة ولو وتر حتى قال يقضي على مذهب أبي حنيفة ا هـ . وفي المجتبى : من جهل فرضية الترتيب لا يجب عليه كالناسي وهو قول جماعة من أئمة بلخ وفي القدوري الكبير : ترك الظهر وصلى العصر ذاكراً فسد ثم قضى الظهر وصلى المغرب قبل إعادة العصر صح مغربه، ولو علم أن عليه إعادة العصر لم تجز مغربه ولم يفصل في الأصل بين ما إذا كان عالماً أو جاهلاً. رحمه الله : وهذا معنى قولهم الفاسد لا يوجب الترتيب. هذا ما ظهر للعبد الضعيف، هذا وقد ذكر في المحيط معزياً إلى النوادر : لو صلى الظهر على ظن أنه متوضيء ثم توضأ وصلى العصر ثم تبين يعيد الظهر خاصة لأنه بمنزلة الناسي في حق الظهر فلم يلزمه مراعاة الترتيب ا هـ. وليس بمخالف لما قدمناه عنهم لأن فيما قدمناه كان وقت العصر ذاكراً أنه صلى الظهر بغير طهارة وفي مسألة النوادر التذكر حصل بعد أداء العصر.
The Book of Prayer, Chapter on Making Up Missed Prayers and Forgetfulness, and their Becoming Six, and It Does Not Return to Fewness. So if one prays a fard prayer while remembering a missed one, even if witr, until he said: 'He makes up according to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa.' In al-Mujtaba: 'Whoever is ignorant of the obligation of order, it is not obligatory upon him, like the forgetful.' This is the saying of a group of scholars from Balkh. In al-Quduri al-Kabir: 'He left Zuhr and prayed Asr while remembering, so it is invalid. Then he made up Zuhr and prayed Maghrib before repeating Asr; his Maghrib is valid. And if he knew that he must repeat Asr, his Maghrib is not permissible.' And it did not distinguish in the original between whether he is knowing or ignorant. He said: 'This is the meaning of their saying: The fasid does not necessitate order.' This is what appeared to the weak servant. Also it is mentioned in al-Muhit, attributed to al-Nawadir: 'If he prayed Zuhr thinking he had wudu, then performed wudu and prayed Asr, then it became clear, he repeats only Zuhr because he is like the forgetful with respect to Zuhr, so he is not required to observe order.' It is not contradictory to what we have presented from them, because in what we presented, at the time of Asr he was remembering that he prayed Zuhr without purification, whereas in the matter of al-Nawadir, the remembrance occurred after performing Asr.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage begins with the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary. It first states the general ruling that if one prays a present fard prayer while intentionally remembering a missed prayer (even witr), the present prayer is invalid. The commentator notes that this is according to Abu Hanifa. Then it cites al-Mujtaba, stating that one who is ignorant of the obligation of order in making up prayers is like the forgetful—order is not required. This is the view of a group of scholars from Balkh. The text then brings a specific case from al-Quduri al-Kabir: if a person leaves Zuhr and then prays Asr while remembering the missed Zuhr, the Asr is invalid. If he then makes up Zuhr and prays Maghrib before repeating Asr, the Maghrib is valid. However, if he knows that he must repeat Asr (i.e., he knows his Asr was invalid), then his Maghrib is not valid. The commentator explains that this illustrates the principle that an invalid (fasid) prayer does not create an obligation of order. Finally, it cites a narration from al-Nawadir in al-Muhit: if one prays Zuhr thinking he has wudu, then performs wudu and prays Asr, and later discovers he had no wudu for Zuhr, he only repeats Zuhr—he is like the forgetful regarding Zuhr, so order is not required. This does not contradict the earlier ruling because in the earlier case the person remembered the missed prayer at the time of Asr, whereas in the Nawadir case the realization came after Asr was completed. Thus, the ruling depends on when the remembrance occurs: if during the present prayer, order is mandatory; if after, it is not.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartib): The obligation to make up missed prayers in their correct chronological order.
الناسي (al-nasi): One who forgets the obligation of order or forgets to make up a prayer; excused from order.
الفاسد (al-fasid): An invalid prayer that must be repeated but does not necessitate order for subsequent prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the present prayer if someone prays it while intentionally remembering a missed prayer?
A) It is valid and no order is required.
B) It is invalid and must be repeated.
C) It is valid because the missed prayer is still in the past.
D) It is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
One who is ignorant of the obligation of order in making up missed prayers is like the forgetful and does not need to observe order; the invalid prayer does not necessitate order.

The Criterion for Dropping Order in Missed Prayers
Explaining the criterion for dropping order and the intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on when the obligation of order (tartib) drops in making up missed prayers and explain the dispute over whether the criterion is the number of missed prayers themselves or the number of intervening times.

Core Idea
Order drops when six missed prayers accumulate, not when six intervening times elapse.

In the Hanafi school, it is wajib to perform missed prayers in order. However, when the number of missed prayers reaches six, the obligation of order is lifted due to hardship. This lesson examines the exact criterion for this exemption: is it based on the missed prayers themselves or on the number of intervening present prayer times? We will explore the textual evidence and the practical difference between the two views.

Guided Path
1
State the default rule and the exception
First, recall that the Hanafi school requires order (tartib) in making up missed prayers. The exception is when the missed prayers become 'kathrah' (abundance). Identify the threshold number according to the relied-upon position.
The default order is wajib; the exception is a rukhsa (concession) to avoid hardship.
2
Identify the two competing criteria
The passage presents two views: (1) The criterion is the missed prayers themselves becoming six; (2) The criterion is the number of intervening present prayer times (awqat mutakhalila) reaching six. Note the authorities who hold each view, e.g., al-Muhit supports view 1, al-Zayla'i supports view 2.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhahib.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof for the relied-upon view
Study the example of missing a full day and dawn: the missed prayers become six at sunrise of the second day without the seventh time entering. This directly shows that six prayers can be missed without six intervening times. The hardship ('haraj') is caused by the number of prayers, not the number of times.
The proof uses a concrete example from the passage.
4
Examine the opposing proof and the Hanafi response
The opposing view says the benefit (sihhat al-waqtiyyah) only accrues when the seventh time enters. The Hanafi response points out that the benefit also includes dropping order among the missed prayers themselves, and that on Abu Hanifa's view, present prayers performed while remembering a missed prayer are invalid, so those intervening times do not count as 'nutlaq' times.
This is the key rebuttal that refutes the fruit case given by al-Zayla'i.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Apply both views to a scenario: one misses Zuhr of day 1, Asr of day 2, and Maghrib of day 3, forgetting the order. Under the 'times' view, order drops because there are many intervening times (e.g., between missing Zuhr and Asr there are several present prayers), so he prays only three. Under the 'prayers' view, order does not drop because only three prayers are missed; he must pray seven (three missed + four present in between). The passage ultimately adopts the 'prayers' view as more correct.
This practical fruit shows why the dispute matters.
